                googletag.pubads().refresh();

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d1aeedd8ca254bc0a16cd41077fb33e1">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '8ba8e7c2-6ad8-4fba-adc6-411c15c9cd6e'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d208434904" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) employ P2-type layered transition metal oxides as promising cathode materials, primarily due to their abundant natural reserves and environmentally friendly characteristics. However, structural instability and complex phase transitions during electrochemical cycling pose significant challenges to their practical applications. Employing cation substitution serves as a straightforward yet effective strategy for stabilizing the structure and improving the kinetics of the active material. In this study, we introduce a Ni-rich honeycomb-layered Na<sub>2+<i>x</i></sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>TeO<sub>6</sub> (NNTO) cathode material with variable sodium content (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.03, 0.05, 0.10). Physicochemical characterizations reveal that excess sodium content at the atomic scale modifies the surface and suppresses phase transitions, while preserving the crystal structure. This results in enhanced cyclic performance and improved electrochemical kinetics at room temperature. Furthermore, we investigate the performance of the NNTO cathode material containing 10% excess sodium at a relatively high temperature of 60°C, where it exhibits 71.6% capacity retention compared to 60% for the pristine. Overall, our results confirm that a preconstructed surface layer (induced by excess sodium) effectively safeguards the Ni-based cathode material from surface degradation and phase transitions during the electrochemical processes, thus exhibiting superior capacity retention relative to the pristine NNTO cathode. This study of the correlation between structure and performance can potentially be applied to the commercialization of SIBs.</p></div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2658-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2658-sec-0010-title">1 INTRODUCTION</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have become an integral component of daily life, especially with the proliferation of portable electronic devices and electric vehicles.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0001_R_d208434796e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat Rev Mater">1</a>-<a href="#cey2658-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0003_R_d208434796e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy">3</a></sup></span> Despite their utility, the high cost and limited availability of lithium resources necessitate the exploration of alternative energy storage technologies.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0004_R_d208434796e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Int J Energy Res">4</a>-<a href="#cey2658-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0008_R_d208434796e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Renewable Sustainable Energy Rev">8</a></sup></span> In this context, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), which share similar energy storage mechanisms with LIBs and benefit from abundant sodium reserves, have emerged as promising candidates for next-generation energy storage systems.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0009_R_d208434796e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv Energy Mater">9</a>-<a href="#cey2658-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0013_R_d208434796e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv Energy Mater">13</a></sup></span> However, the cathode material remains a critical factor affecting both the performance and manufacturing cost of batteries. Furthermore, undesirable phase transitions during electrochemical processes compromise cycle life and energy density. Therefore, the development of high-performance cathode materials is essential for the practical implementation of SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0012_R_d208434796e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Small">12</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0014_R_d208434796e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Energy Lett">14</a>-<a href="#cey2658-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0024_R_d208434796e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Sci China Chem">24</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In the quest to find cathode materials that offer high energy density and exceptional electrochemical performance, honeycomb-layered structures with the composition Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>(MM′)O<sub>2</sub> (where M = Ni, Co, Zn and M′ = Sb, Te, Nb, etc.) have garnered considerable attention<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0025_R_d208434796e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem Soc Rev">25</a>-<a href="#cey2658-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0030_R_d208434796e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30">30</a></sup></span> and are considered optimal candidates for SIBs. It is thought that this family of honeycomb-layered oxides can achieve higher energy density than conventional Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MO<sub>2</sub> cathodes.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0027_R_d208434796e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J Mater Chem A">27</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0031_R_d208434796e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J Power Sources">31</a>-<a href="#cey2658-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0034_R_d208434796e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Solid State Ionics">34</a></sup></span> Partial substitution of transition metals (M) with high-valency metals (M′) enhances both high-voltage performance and thermal stability. This&nbsp;substitution renders the M oxide bonds more ionized, thereby requiring additional energy for the oxidation of M cations during Na-ion removal. As a result, P2-type honeycomb-layered structures such as Ni-rich Na<sub>2</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>TeO<sub>6</sub> (NNTO) can operate at high voltages, approximately 4.5 V, compared to Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MO<sub>2</sub> cathodes.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0031_R_d208434796e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J Power Sources">31</a>-<a href="#cey2658-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0033_R_d208434796e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv Mater">33</a></sup></span> NNTO has been successfully employed in SIBs as a host framework for the reversible insertion of Na ions. Significant advancements were reported by Gupta et al., who observed a smooth charge and discharge profile with a capacity of 90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05 C.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0035_R_d208434796e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J Power Sources">35</a></sup></span> Conversely, Pati et al. noted a stair-like galvanostatic profile in NNTO electrodes, attributing it to stacking disorders in the honeycomb-layered structure; these disorders were correlated with phase transitions, capacity decay, and structural collapse.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0036_R_d208434796e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Materialia">36</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0037_R_d208434796e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J Mater Chem A">37</a></sup></span> The effects of such structural anomalies on the functionality of layered materials can be either detrimental or beneficial.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0036_R_d208434796e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Materialia">36</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0038_R_d208434796e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ChemRxiv">38</a>-<a href="#cey2658-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0041_R_d208434796e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Inorg Chem">41</a></sup></span> Consequently, both theoretical and experimental investigations are warranted to elucidate the relationship between atomic-scale structural disorder, electrochemical performance, and phase transitions.</p>
         
         <p>To address the challenges of structural defects or stacking disorders and to enhance the electrochemical performance of these layered structures, various strategies such as surface coating, morphology design, and cation substitution have been proposed.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0027_R_d208434796e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J Mater Chem A">27</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0031_R_d208434796e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J Power Sources">31</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0032_R_d208434796e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem Commun">32</a></sup></span> Among these, cation substitution stands as a straightforward and effective method to improve Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics and structural stability. Nevertheless, existing cathodes such as Na<sub>2</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>TeO<sub>6</sub> <sup>30</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>Ni<sub>2−<i>x</i></sub>Co<sub><i>x</i></sub>TeO<sub>6</sub><span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0027_R_d208434796e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J Mater Chem A">27</a></sup></span> continue to experience detrimental phase transitions and poor capacity retention. In this context, we synthesized P2-Na<sub>2+<i>x</i></sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>TeO<sub>6</sub> (NNTO) with varying levels of excess sodium content (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.03, 0.05, and 0.10) and examined the impact of excess sodium on morphology, crystal structure, and cyclic performance at temperatures of 25°C and 60°C. Our investigation revealed the presence of structural disorders in the P2-NNTO material at the higher sodium content (i.e., at <i>x</i> = 0.05 and 0.1). Detailed analysis confirmed that these defects are advantageous for the NNTO crystal structure. Specifically, they aid in mitigating adverse phase transitions, minimizing Na<sup>+</sup>-vacancy ordering, and thereby enhancing reversible capacity, rate performance, and cycle stability. Our study showed that the NNTO cathode with <i>x</i> = 0.10 exhibited remarkable stability during electrochemical cycling. The notable findings from our comprehensive analyses highlight its exceptional diffusion kinetics and its potential utility as a stable electrode material.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2658-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2658-sec-0020-title">2 RESULTS AND DISCUSSION</h2>
         
         <p>P2-type Na<sub>2</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>TeO<sub>6</sub> (NNTO-0) free from impurity phases was successfully synthesized using the high-temperature solid-state synthesis method. Its X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern is presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1A</a> (black line). XRD patterns of sodium-enriched honeycomb-layered oxides Na<sub>2+<i>x</i></sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>TeO<sub>6</sub> for <i>x</i> = 0.03, 0.05, and 0.10, designated as NNTO-3 (red line), NNTO-5 (blue line), and NNTO-10 (green line), respectively, are also illustrated in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1A</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>. All the XRD patterns indicate that these NNTO compounds possess a P2-type layered structure with a hexagonal phase; the majority of their peaks are in close alignment with the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mcm</i> space group (PDF # 00-58-0052).<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0037_R_d208434796e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J Mater Chem A">37</a></sup></span> It is noteworthy that in the XRD pattern of NNTO-0, reflections with even <i>l</i> indices exhibit higher intensities compared to those with odd <i>l</i> indices. This pattern implies that the atoms (Ni and Te) are arranged at repeated distances of c/2, with Ni situated above Ni and Te above Te,<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0036_R_d208434796e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Materialia">36</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0042_R_d208434796e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Chem Mater">42</a></sup></span> as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1C</a>. In contrast, for NNTO-3, NNTO-5, and NNTO-10, an additional weak peak (101) located at 21.2° is also discernible in the XRD patterns (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1B</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>). While this peak is forbidden in the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mcm</i> space group, it is only permissible in the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>22 space group. This additional peak suggests the occasional presence of structural disorders or stacking faults along the c-axis.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0036_R_d208434796e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Materialia">36</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0042_R_d208434796e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Chem Mater">42</a></sup></span> It is important to note that a sodium content higher than <i>x</i> = 0.1 leads to a phase change from the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mcm</i> to the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>22 space group, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>. Moreover, the (002) diffraction peak at 2<i>θ</i> = 15.9° for sodium-enriched NNTO compounds shifts toward a lower angle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1B</a>), indicative of increased interlayer spacing within the layered structure (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>). Alterations in the lattice parameters of <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mcm</i>, originating from the different sodium contents, are documented in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>. This table confirms an expansion in the c-axis lattice parameter values, attributed to a rise in the <i>d</i>-spacing between the metal oxide layers (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>). Concurrently, a reduction in the lattice parameter “a” for NNTO-10 also signifies a shortened path for Na<sup>+</sup> ions, potentially facilitating improved ion diffusion within the bulk phase.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0043_R_d208434796e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - ACS Omega">43</a>-<a href="#cey2658-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0045_R_d208434796e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Mater Today">45</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cey2658-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f0da2a1c-7b34-4d42-8e75-150eca519dc9/cey2658-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f0da2a1c-7b34-4d42-8e75-150eca519dc9/cey2658-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8e6ca766-dce3-4363-9b3a-184a5b4fcf71/cey2658-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f0da2a1c-7b34-4d42-8e75-150eca519dc9/cey2658-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2658-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.658" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterization of P2-NNTO cathode material. (A, B) XRD patterns of NNTO-0, NNTO-3, NNTO-5, and NNTO-10. (C) Schematic diagram of the NNTO crystal structure with <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mcm</i> phase. (D–G) SEM images of NNTO-0 and NNTO-10 showing the effect of sodium content on the morphology of particles, along with their respective average particle size. HR-TEM images of (H) NNTO-0 and (I) NNTO-10 and their corresponding (J) SAD and (K) FFT patterns, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To investigate the impact of sodium content on the morphology of NNTO powders, scanning electron microscopy (SEM) was employed. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1D–G</a>, NNTO exhibits varying morphologies with increasing sodium concentrations. The well-ordered cathode material NNTO-0, depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1D</a>, exhibits particle sizes ranging from 2 to 11 μm, with an average particle size of 5.02 μm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1E</a><b>)</b>. Further, horizontal striations are visible on the facets of these larger, hexagonal-shaped NNTO-0 platelets, separated by distances ranging from tens to hundreds of nanometers (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>). In contrast, NNTO-10 (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1F,G</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>) has an average particle size of 1.14 μm, while NNTO-3 and NNTO-5 have average particle sizes of 1.42 and 1.28 μm, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>). SEM imaging also indicates that the thickness of smaller particles has increased in NNTO-10 (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>) relative to NNTO-0, likely owing to the expansion of the c-axis in these structures.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0046_R_d208434796e698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - ACS Appl Mater Interfaces">46</a></sup></span> It should be emphasized that these alterations in lattice parameters and particle sizes are solely induced by the increased sodium content. This variation in the stoichiometry may reasonably alter the surface energy of the crystal planes, which influence the growth rate of different facets, promote the formation of layered structures with more {010} facets, and increase the thickness of particles along the c-axis,<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0047_R_d208434796e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nat Commun">47</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0048_R_d208434796e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv Mater">48</a></sup></span> as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a> for NNTO-10. Additionally, energy-dispersive spectrometry (EDS) mappings of NNTO-0 and NNTO-10, presented in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, respectively, affirm the uniform distribution of Na, Ni, Te, and O throughout the lattice in the bulk material.</p>
         
         <p>The influence of excess sodium content on the local structures and chemical distributions of NNTO is further elucidated using transmission electron microscopy (TEM), combined with selected area diffraction (SAD) patterns, fast fourier transform (FFT) and high-resolution (HR) images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1H–K</a>). Large bright grains can be observed in the HR-TEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1J</a>), which, when projected along the [1<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/435636aa-e8ba-47c2-ae58-22a0592da66e/cey2658-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:10973923:media:cey2658:cey2658-math-0001" location="graphic/cey2658-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mover>
                        <mn>1</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> &lt;math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" altimg="urn:x-wiley:10973923:media:cey2658:cey2658-math-0001" wiley:location="equation/cey2658-math-0001.png"&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mover&gt;&lt;mn&gt;1&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x000AF}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;/mover&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/math&gt;</annotation>
               </semantics></math>0] zone axis, represent columns of transition-metal atoms (Ni and Te) in NNTO-0. These columns are consistent with the P2-type honeycomb layered oxide structure within the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mcm</i> space group.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0036_R_d208434796e739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Materialia">36</a></sup></span> Moreover, the HR-TEM image of NNTO-0 reveals a lattice fringe distance of 0.556 nm corresponding to (002) planes, which is consistent with our XRD results. Conversely, the HR-TEM image of NNTO-10 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1I</a>) shows the presence of a disordered, preconstructed surface layer on the active NNTO material in the presence of excess sodium. The thickness of this preconstructed surface layer is about 6.9 nm for NNTO-10 and is highly dependent on the content of sodium in the NNTO crystal structure, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>. The SAD pattern of NNTO-0 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1J</a>) displays a well-ordered structure with Te atoms vertically aligned above or below adjacent slabs, as can be seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1C</a>. In contrast, in NNTO-10 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1K</a>), the presence of excess sodium ions in the ab plane causes deviation from vertical alignment, signifying the coexistence of structural disorders or stacking faults along the stacking direction (i.e., the c-axis).<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0036_R_d208434796e758" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Materialia">36</a></sup></span> The excess sodium not only increases the interatomic distance within the NNTO-10 bulk to 0.559 nm—favorable for Na-ion de/insertion during cycling—but also forms a beneficial preconstructed layer that enhances the structural stability of the cathode material against atmospheric conditions.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0049_R_d208434796e761" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nat Commun">49</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0050_R_d208434796e761" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Nat Commun">50</a></sup></span> Subsequent stability tests for NNTO-0 and NNTO-10 were also conducted, and their ex-situ XRD patterns were analyzed after 1 day of exposure to air and moisture. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> confirms the structural reconstruction in NNTO-0 due to reactive Na and Ni species, whereas NNTO-10 displays excellent stability, attributed to its preconstructed layer that also acts as a protective surface layer.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of the NNTO series was evaluated using a half-cell configuration within a voltage range of 3.0–4.5 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2A–C</a> presents a comparison of galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) profiles for NNTO cathodes at a rate of 0.1 C. NNTO-0, NNTO-3, NNTO-5, and NNTO-10 samples with differing sodium contents exhibit first-charge capacities of 102, 104.8, 106.2, and 109.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2A</a>. In the subsequent discharge process, capacities of 80.4, 84.1, 85.6, and 88.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> were achieved, with Coulombic efficiencies of 78.8%, 80.2%, 80.6%, and 81.0%, respectively. While the charge–discharge capacity follows a trend similar to sodium content, all cathodes experience a significant irreversible loss of capacity following the initial charge. This loss may be attributed to kinetic barriers that impede sodium reinsertion into the structure.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0037_R_d208434796e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J Mater Chem A">37</a></sup></span> The multiple plateaus in the voltage profile of NNTO-0, especially the stair-like features, indicate complex phase transitions that could contribute to this irreversible capacity loss.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0032_R_d208434796e790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem Commun">32</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0037_R_d208434796e790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J Mater Chem A">37</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0045_R_d208434796e790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Mater Today">45</a></sup></span> It is also observed that the plateaus within the 3.6–4.2 V range for NNTO-0 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2B</a>) diminish and eventually vanish with increasing sodium content, as shown for NNTO-5 (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2A</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>) and NNTO-10 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2C</a>). This alteration could be due to a volume expansion in the NNTO crystal structure. Additionally, the transition in particle size from micro- to sub-micro–sized particles and the formation of the surface layer could play a significant role in NNTO-10, as smaller particles offer a larger specific surface area.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0045_R_d208434796e806" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Mater Today">45</a></sup></span> In addition, the comparison of the voltage hysteresis for both NNTO-0 and NNTO-10 (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>) confirmed that the formation of preconstructed surface layer also decreases the energy losses caused by the effects of polarization produced at high voltage,<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0051_R_d208434796e812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Sci Rep">51</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0052_R_d208434796e812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J Am Chem Soc">52</a></sup></span> thereby improving its electrochemical performance compared to other Ni-rich cathode materials (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0053_R_d208434796e818" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Chem Commun">53</a>-<a href="#cey2658-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0062_R_d208434796e818" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - J Mater Chem A">62</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cey2658-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eeab6ca8-bbd9-40a3-8a26-b6368da473cc/cey2658-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/eeab6ca8-bbd9-40a3-8a26-b6368da473cc/cey2658-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/37af302c-ab48-4763-9730-d745107d034b/cey2658-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eeab6ca8-bbd9-40a3-8a26-b6368da473cc/cey2658-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2658-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.658" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of NNTO cathode material. (A) Comparison of the first charge and discharge cycle of NNTO-0, NNTO-3, NNTO-5, and NNTO-10 at a rate of 0.1 C in a voltage window of 3.0–4.5 V. The first five cycles of (B) NNTO-0 and (C) NNTO-10 show a transition from stair-like GCD profile to sloppy curves, along with their corresponding (D, E) CV curves measured at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. (F) The rate-performance comparison at various C-rates and (G) long-term cycling stability of NNTO cathodes at a rate of 0.1 C at 25°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To investigate and compare the mechanisms of sodium insertion and extraction in NNTO cathodes, cyclic voltammetry (CV) was performed in a half-cell configuration. Three CV cycles for both NNTO-0 and NNTO-10 are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2D,E</a>, at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, displaying multiple redox peaks within the voltage range of 3.0–4.5 V. The principal oxidation peaks for NNTO-0 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2D</a>) appear at 3.73, 3.98, 4.16, and 4.32 V, and the corresponding reduction peaks are observed at 3.45, 3.64, 3.83, and 3.98 V. These peaks signify multi-electron redox reactions associated with the Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> oxidation and reduction processes.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0035_R_d208434796e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J Power Sources">35</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0037_R_d208434796e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J Mater Chem A">37</a></sup></span> Notably, splitting in some of the anodic and cathodic peaks is evident in the CV curves, indicating either interlayer sliding of transition metal layers or varying degrees of sodium disorder in NNTO-0.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0037_R_d208434796e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J Mater Chem A">37</a></sup></span> Electrochemical sodium extraction results in slab gliding within the honeycomb structure, leading to modifications in the crystal structure during sodium atom rearrangement.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0040_R_d208434796e865" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Sci Adv">40</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0041_R_d208434796e865" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Inorg Chem">41</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0063_R_d208434796e865" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv Energy Mater">63</a></sup></span> These rearrangements induce phase transitions, resulting in a stair-like voltage profile.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0045_R_d208434796e868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Mater Today">45</a></sup></span> Interestingly, major oxidation peaks attributed to inter-slab gliding in the CV of NNTO-0 diminish and eventually disappear in the CV profiles of NNTO-3 (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c</a>), NNTO-5 (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9d</a>), and NNTO-10 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2E</a>). Specifically, two notable anodic peaks at 3.69 and 4.4 V, along with a significant cathodic peak at 3.69 V, emerge for the sodium-excess NNTO-10. The peaks between 3.6 and 4.2 V are ascribed to the Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> redox reactions during sodium-ion extraction and insertion, while the anodic peak at 4.4 V may be related to high-voltage electrolyte oxidation.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0032_R_d208434796e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem Commun">32</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0035_R_d208434796e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J Power Sources">35</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0037_R_d208434796e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J Mater Chem A">37</a></sup></span> The reduction in plateaus upon extraction and insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> for cathode materials reflects the decrease in diffusion path due to changes in the lattice parameters and the size of particles, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0001">1</a>.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0021_R_d208434796e894" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J Mater Sci Technol">21</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0033_R_d208434796e894" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv Mater">33</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0045_R_d208434796e894" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Mater Today">45</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The rate performances of all NNTO cathodes at various C-rates are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2F</a>. NNTO-10 exhibits the highest discharge capacity and superior cyclic stability, affirming the improved kinetics originating from the reduced diffusion pathways along the a-axis (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>). A comparison of cyclic performance for all NNTO samples at a rate of 0.1 C is presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2G</a>. Discharge capacities reveal that NNTO-10 has superior capacity retention compared to NNTO-0, NNTO-3, and NNTO-5. After 100 cycles, capacity retention is 71.3% for NNTO-0 and 72.1%, 74.2%, and 76.7% for NNTO-3, NNTO-5, and NNTO-10, respectively. Hence, these observations confirm that a reduced diffusion path in NNTO-10, along with an increase in c/a ratio after reducing the particle size from micro to sub-micro meter in the presence of excess sodium ions, could effectively improve the sodium-ion diffusion, resulting in enhanced cycle stability and rate performance of the NNTO-10 cathode material. Additionally, the emergence of a preconstructed surface structure may contribute to maintaining structural stability.</p>
         
         <p>To elucidate the impact of excess sodium on the oxidation states of nickel, both X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectroscopy and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) were employed. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0003">3A</a> displays the Ni K-edge XANES spectra of NNTO-0, NNTO-3, NNTO-5, and NNTO-10, all of which resemble that of the reference material, indicating the divalent oxidation state of Ni in NNTO. In NNTO-0 (represented by the black line), a slightly higher oxidation state of Ni was observed compared to NNTO-5 (blue line) and NNTO-10 (green line). A minor shift toward lower energy levels was noticed with an increase in sodium content, as illustrated in the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0003">3A</a>. Notably, XPS results (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>) corroborate these findings, demonstrating that the excess sodium content induces only a nominal shift in the Ni 2p core-level spectra toward lower binding energy. This suggests that the oxidation states of Ni remain largely unaffected with increased sodium content in the NNTO structure.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cey2658-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0a58cdd0-4b1e-49f4-9384-5469b9b3df5a/cey2658-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0a58cdd0-4b1e-49f4-9384-5469b9b3df5a/cey2658-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6c4dfb93-7b3c-43ce-be74-97378ed15b70/cey2658-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0a58cdd0-4b1e-49f4-9384-5469b9b3df5a/cey2658-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2658-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.658" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Effect of excess sodium on the oxidation state of Ni. (A) Normalized XANES spectra and (B) Fourier-transformed EXAFS spectra at the Ni K-edge of NNTO cathode materials. XANES spectra of NNTO-0 and NNTO-10 exhibit Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> redox reactions during the (C) charge and (D) discharge processes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Further insights into the effects of sodium excess on NNTO structure were gleaned through Fourier transforms (FTs) of the Ni K-edge extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectroscopy, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0003">3B</a>. The peak at shorter distances (between 1 and 2 Å) is attributed to the Ni-to-O single scattering path, corresponding to the first shell of metal–oxygen bonds.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0031_R_d208434796e952" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J Power Sources">31</a></sup></span> Peaks at 2.6 Å are ascribed to the second shell involving Ni-to-Ni/Te bonds within the same ab plane.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0031_R_d208434796e955" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J Power Sources">31</a></sup></span> Prior studies have reported that a minor increase in bond length (Ni–O) is directly related to the reduction in particle size,<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0044_R_d208434796e958" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J Am Chem Soc">44</a></sup></span> a phenomenon evidenced in NNTO-10 (green line). Such minor changes in bond lengths, induced by excess sodium in the NNTO crystal structure, could potentially contribute to residual stress and the fragmentation of larger particles into smaller counterparts. To elucidate the charge compensation mechanism, ex-situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) was performed on both NNTO-0 and NNTO-10 during the charge–discharge cycles. Shifts in the Ni K-edge toward higher energy regions were observed, indicative of the oxidation of Ni<sup>2+</sup> to Ni<sup>3+</sup> during the charging process, as presented in the XANES spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0003">3C</a>. Conversely, the Ni K-edge shifted back during the discharge process, as evident in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0003">3D</a>. Therefore, it can be confirmed that in both NNTO-0 and NNTO-10 cathodes, charge compensation is facilitated exclusively through the Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> redox couple, a finding that aligns closely with the CV data presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2</a>.</p>
         
         <p>To gain a deeper insight into the effect of excess sodium on structural transformations during electrochemical cycling, in situ XRD analyses were conducted on NNTO cathodes at a rate of 0.1 C. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0004">4</a> shows the comparative in situ XRD patterns for NNTO-0 and NNTO-10, each corresponding to distinct GCD profiles previously illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0002">2</a>. Detailed XRD patterns for these materials are provided in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>. For NNTO-0, the (002) diffraction peak manifests a consistent shift toward lower angles during the charging process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0004">4A</a>). This behavior signifies an elongation in the c-axis and a contraction of the ab-plane, a phenomenon attributable to increased electrostatic repulsion between adjacent oxygen layers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0004">4</a>). Interestingly, this peak exhibits reversibility upon reinsertion of sodium during the discharge process. Similar structural evolution was also performed for NNTO-10 (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0004">4B</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>) during the charge–discharge process. The peaks corresponding to the (002), (004), and (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b0dcf9c1-de7b-4c2b-8124-740dcf3573c4/cey2658-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:10973923:media:cey2658:cey2658-math-0002" location="graphic/cey2658-math-0002.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mn>2</mn>
                     <mover>
                        <mn>1</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                     <mn>0</mn>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> &lt;math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" altimg="urn:x-wiley:10973923:media:cey2658:cey2658-math-0002" wiley:location="equation/cey2658-math-0002.png"&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mn&gt;2&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;mover&gt;&lt;mn&gt;1&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x000AF}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;/mover&gt;&lt;mn&gt;0&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/math&gt;</annotation>
               </semantics></math>) planes underwent minor initial shifts but stabilized in successive charge and discharge cycles, affirming the stability of the lattice parameters (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0004">4D</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12d</a>). This suggests that sodium extraction from NNTO-0 follows a quasi-solid-solution reaction mechanism, whereas NNTO-10 shows complete solid-solution behavior consistent with its GCD profile.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cey2658-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c555c300-ed0e-46e6-a282-b3067e3b2d70/cey2658-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c555c300-ed0e-46e6-a282-b3067e3b2d70/cey2658-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9e16f447-a1e0-42fc-b6c3-da6e50a4f08b/cey2658-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c555c300-ed0e-46e6-a282-b3067e3b2d70/cey2658-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2658-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.658" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In-situ structure characterization of NNTO cathodes. In-situ XRD patterns demonstrating the changes in the (002) peak for (A) NNTO-0 and (B) NNTO-10 during their charge and discharge processes between 3.0 and 4.5 V at a rate of 0.1 C, along with the corresponding (C, D) changes in their lattice parameters and volume, respectively. (E) Comparison of the GITT profiles of NNTO-0 and NNTO-10 at a rate of 0.1 C. (F, H) The alkali-ion diffusion coefficient, and (G, I) over-potential (<i>η</i>) corresponding to the resistance of NNTO in different states during the charge and discharge processes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To gain a better understanding of this process, a galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was employed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0004">4E</a>). The GITT curve for NNTO-0 (represented by the red line) displays multiple plateaus at 3.6, 3.9, and 4.2 V, indicating high polarization during charging. In stark contrast, NNTO-10 (green line) revealed a single-phase reaction across a potential range of 3.0–4.25 V, which can be ascribed to reduced diffusion pathways and the presence of a pre-formed surface structure facilitated by excess sodium. GITT analysis was also used to calculate the sodium diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na+</sub>) for both NNTO materials across various potentials (Note&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>). The observed values of D<sub>Na+</sub> for both NNTO-0 and NNTO-10 were approximately 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> across the measured open-circuit voltages. NNTO-0 exhibits a notable decrease in D<sub>Na+</sub> at around 3.6 V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0004">4F</a>), likely attributed to sluggish electrochemical kinetics resulting from either phase transitions or metal layer gliding post sodium-ion extraction.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0064_R_d208434796e1069" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Adv Funct Mater">64</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0065_R_d208434796e1069" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Electrochim Acta">65</a></sup></span> Upon further extraction, both materials display increased polarization with voltage, reaching up to 4.5 V; this is thought to be linked to electrolyte decomposition at elevated voltages.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0035_R_d208434796e1072" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J Power Sources">35</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0037_R_d208434796e1072" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J Mater Chem A">37</a></sup></span> Consequently, there is an increase in over-potential (<i>η</i>) at this stage (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0004">4G</a>). A significant reduction in <i>η</i> was confirmed for NNTO-10 at these high cut-off voltages, affirming the decreased charge-transfer resistance owing to the protective surface-layer formation. During discharge, a substantial drop in D<sub>Na+</sub> occurs at 3.8 V for both materials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0004">4H</a>), corresponding to redox reactions within the active material and signaling poor diffusion kinetics upon sodium reinsertion.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0066_R_d208434796e1088" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - J Electrochem Sci Technol">66</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0067_R_d208434796e1088" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - Sci Technol Adv Mater">67</a></sup></span> Finally, the lower internal resistance observed for NNTO-10 relative to NNTO-0 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0004">4I</a>) suggests that NNTO-0 experiences inhibited diffusion kinetics, either due to phase transitions or due to increased disorder during cycling. Conversely, the diffusion kinetics in NNTO-10 are conducive to significant capacity retention over multiple cycles. Furthermore, the comparison of the EIS spectra for NNTO cathodes (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>) confirms that the formation of a protective surface layer in the presence of excess sodium suppresses the structural disorder and interfacial degradation during cycling, thereby, decreasing the kinetic barrier for reversible sodium-ion insertion and enhancing the cycling stability and rate capability.</p>
         
         <p>In summary, the comparative investigation between NNTO-0 and NNTO-10 strongly indicates that the presence of a preconstructed surface layer significantly contributes to structural stability and mitigates the propensity for side reactions at elevated cut-off voltages, specifically 4.5 V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0005">5A</a>). Previous studies on Ni-rich active materials have shown that cathode surface degradation due to electrolyte decomposition and oxidation can result in transition-metal dissolution and surface reconstruction.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0068_R_d208434796e1104" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - ACS Energy Lett">68</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0069_R_d208434796e1104" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 69">69</a></sup></span> As a result, surface modification emerges as an effective strategy for preventing transition-metal dissolution from cathodes, as it provides a protective layer that guards against interfacial degradation.<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0070_R_d208434796e1107" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - Nano Lett">70</a>, <a href="#cey2658-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0071_R_d208434796e1107" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 71 - J Mater Chem A">71</a></sup></span> Herein, a straightforward, one-step synthesis incorporating such preconstructed surface modifications on sodium-excess NNTO has demonstrably improved the electrochemical performance of the NNTO cathode material.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cey2658-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/85373bf6-fbb1-43f2-8cc4-a42ed355c0be/cey2658-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/85373bf6-fbb1-43f2-8cc4-a42ed355c0be/cey2658-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f32835b5-c3cc-4996-8812-3b7a41d00be6/cey2658-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/85373bf6-fbb1-43f2-8cc4-a42ed355c0be/cey2658-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2658-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.658" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">NNTO electrochemical performance at high temperature. (A) A schematic of NNTO illustrating the protective effect of the preconstructed surface layer on the bulk structure. Charge and discharge curves for (B) NNTO-0 and (C) NNTO-10 at a rate of 0.1 C and temperature of 60°C in the voltage range of 3.0–4.5 V along with (D) their corresponding cyclic stability.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Furthermore, to elucidate the relationship between electrochemical performance and operating temperature, GCD profiles were examined within a voltage window of 3.0–4.5 V, at a rate of 0.1 C and an elevated temperature of 60°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2658-fig-0005">5B–D</a>). At this temperature, NNTO-0 maintains its characteristic stair-like voltage profile, indicative of electrochemically driven quasi-phase transitions, with charge and discharge capacities of 141.2 and 72.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In contrast, NNTO-10 displays a sloping voltage profile at 60°C, accompanied by superior charge (161.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and discharge (73.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) capacities, confirming the significant impact of excess sodium ions in the structure. NNTO-10 achieves 71.6% capacity retention after 10 cycles, compared to 60% for NNTO-0. These results strongly indicate that the preconstructed surface layer not only offers effective protection against undesirable side reactions at elevated temperatures but also improves the electrochemical performance of the NNTO cathode material.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2658-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2658-sec-0030-title">3 CONCLUSION</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have successfully synthesized a P2–Na<sub>2+<i>x</i></sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>TeO<sub>6</sub> cathode material characterized by high sodium content and modulated bulk and surface structures. The elevated sodium content in NNTO serves to regulate the Ni–O bond length, thus facilitating enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> mobility, particularly in sodium-deficient states. Notably, the particle size of active material is decreased from 5.02 to 1.14 μm with an increase in the sodium content. Moreover, the excess sodium induced a reconstruction of the surface, forming a stable, protective layer that inhibited phase transformation and structure degradation. This contributed to the structure stability upon Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction during charge–discharge cycling. The P2–Na<sub>2.1</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>TeO<sub>6</sub> (NNTO-10) delivered a reversible discharge capacity of approximately 88.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a rate of 0.1 C within the voltage range of 3.0–4.5 V at 25°C. Additionally, NNTO-10 exhibited a discharge capacity of 73.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 60°C, showing a capacity retention of 71.6%, in comparison to the pristine NNTO-0 cathode material, which could only maintain a 60% capacity retention. These encouraging results highlight the considerable potential of sodium- and nickel-rich NNTO materials for practical application in SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2658-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2658-sec-0040-title">4 EXPERIMENTAL SECTION</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2658-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2658-sec-0050-title">4.1 Methods</h3>
            
            <p>Honeycomb Na<sub>2</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>TeO<sub>6</sub> layered material was synthesized without any impurities using<span><sup><a href="#cey2658-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0053_R_d208434796e1195" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Chem Commun">53</a>-<a href="#cey2658-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2658-bib-0062_R_d208434796e1195" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - J Mater Chem A">62</a></sup></span> a solid-state high-temperature route. Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, NiO, and TeO<sub>2</sub> were used in a stoichiometric ratio as starting materials. Before use, all precursors were heated to 200°C overnight to remove any residual moisture. The precursors were first ground to fine powder and then heated to 650°C for 12 h, followed by annealing at 900°C in air for another 12 h. For the preparation of NNTO with an excess content of sodium, 3%, 5%, and 10% excess of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> were used to synthesize NNTO-3, NNTO-5, and NNTO-10, respectively. After the sintering process, the powders were kept in a dry room to prevent them from absorbing moisture.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2658-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2658-sec-0060-title">4.2 Electrochemistry</h3>
            
            <p>NNTO electrodes were prepared with the active material, acetylene black carbon as a conducting network, and polyvinylidene fluoride as a binder in a mass ratio of 70:20:10. To prepare a uniform slurry, <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone was utilized as the solvent. The slurry was coated on an Al-foil, and was dried in a vacuum oven at 120°C overnight to remove the solvent and residuals. Sodium-metal foil was used as a counter electrode, while the electrolyte comprised 1 M of NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate and fluoroethylene carbonate in a ratio of 98:2 by wt.%. All electrochemical properties were tested using a multichannel battery tester (Maccor version 4000), performed at either room temperature (25°C) or high temperature (60°C). The cut-off voltages for the NNTO cathode in the half-cells were 3.0–4.5 V. To ensure complete electrolyte absorption into the electrode, the sealed cells were aged for 6 h. CV curves were acquired through a Biologic potentiostat/galvanostat model VMP3 (BioLab, Inc.) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. All the CR2032 coin-half-cells were fabricated in an Ar-filled glove box with H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> of less than 0.01 ppm.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2658-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2658-sec-0070-title">4.3 Material characterization</h3>
            
            <p>Ex-situ XRD of the NNTO cathode series was characterized by R-AXIS IV<sup>++</sup>, Rigaku, with Mo-Kα radiation (wavelength of 0.7107 Å) to monitor changes in the crystal lattice and its phase. All powder samples were prepared inside a dry room and covered with Kapton tape before measurement. To study the structure evolution during cycling, in-situ XRD was performed on the samples. The obtained XRD patterns were converted to Cu-Kα radiation for a quick comparison with the literature. The lattice parameters of crystal structures were optimized using Match powder diffraction software. For the stability test, powder samples were kept in open air outside the dry room with a humidity level of approximately 60% for 1 day, and ex-situ XRD was measured at Ultima IV X-ray diffractometer (Rigaku) with monochromatic Cu Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 0.15406 nm) in the 2<i>θ</i> range of 10–80° at a scan rate of 0.02° s<sup>−1</sup>. The surface morphology of the synthesized powders and EDS mapping were acquired using a field emission scanning electron microscope (FE-SEM; S-4200, Hitachi, Japan). Atomic-scale high-angle annular dark field transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-TEM) images, along with HR-TEM images and SAD patterns, were obtained using an FEI Titan TM 80 − 300 microscope at 300 kV. XAS analyses were performed at Ni K-edge with the 1D KIST-PAL bending magnet beamline at the Pohang Accelerated Laboratory. Total electron yield mode was used to obtain all the spectra at a base pressure of 3 × 10<sup>−10</sup> Torr and 0.01 eV. To observe the oxidation states of nickel, a PHI 5000 Versa Probe (Ulvac-PHI) equipped with a monochromator of Al-Kα with an energy of 1486.6 eV was used. GITT measurements were performed with a current density of 0.1 C-rate, where an electrode was subjected to a 10-min current pulse before an hour-long open circuit relaxation. EIS tests were conducted using a Bio-Logic VMP3 from 1 MHz to 0.1 Hz. VESTA software was used to draw the NNTO crystal structure.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="cey2658-bib-0005"><span class="bullet">5</span><span class="author">Nitta N</span>, <span class="author">Wu F</span>, <span class="author">Lee JT</span>, <span class="author">Yushin G</span>. <span class="articleTitle">Li-ion battery materials: present and future</span>. <i>Mater Today</i>. <span class="pubYear">2015</span>; <span class="vol">18</span>(<span class="citedIssue">5</span>): <span class="pageFirst">252</span>-<span class="pageLast">264</span>.
